The Fifth Principle: Divine Hearing and Sight as Perfections
Ghazali defines the fifth principle: Allah's hearing and sight as necessary perfections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers divine hearing and sight necessary perfections and how he argues for them using reason and the example of Prophet Ibrahim.

Core Idea
Divine hearing and sight are perfections that cannot be absent from the Creator.

Ghazali presents the fifth principle of Islamic belief: that Allah is Hearing and Seeing. He begins by describing the reach of these attributes — nothing escapes His sight or hearing. Then he offers a rational argument: if hearing and sight are perfections, how could the Creator lack what He gives to creatures? Finally, he invokes the example of Prophet Ibrahim to show that denying these attributes would undermine the very proof of monotheism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the original Arabic and the English translation side by side. Notice the powerful imagery of a black ant on a black rock in a dark night to emphasize Allah's encompassing perception.
2
Identify the central argument
Ghazali argues that hearing and sight are perfections; therefore, the Creator must possess them to a greater degree than creatures. Find the rhetorical questions that make this point.
The questions are: 'How can the creature be more perfect than the Creator?' and 'How can the division be balanced?'
3
Analyze the Ibrahim example
Why does Ghazali use Ibrahim's argument against idolaters? He shows that if Allah lacked hearing and sight, the same criticism would apply, making Ibrahim's proof invalid.
Ibrahim said to his father: 'Why do you worship that which cannot hear, nor see, nor avail you in anything?'
4
Synthesize the lesson
Now that you've understood the passage, explain in your own words why these attributes are necessary for Allah.
Focus on the perfection argument: since hearing and sight are perfections, the All-Perfect Creator must possess them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الخامس : السمع والبصر
العلم بأنه تعالى سميع بصير لا يعزب عن رؤيته هواجس الضمير وخفايا الوهم والتفكير، ولا يشذ عن سمعه صوت دبيب النملة السوداء في الليلة الظلماء على الصخرة الصماء، وكيف لا يكون سميعاً بصيراً والسمع والبصر كمال لا محالة وليس بنقص؟ فكيف يكون المخلوق أكمل من الخالق الصانع؟ والمصنوع أسنى وأتم من الصانع؟ أو كيف تعتدل القسمة مهما وقع النقص في جهته والكمال في خلقه وصنعته؟ أو كيف تستقيم حجة إبراهيم على أبيه إذ كان يعبد الأصنام جهلاً وغيا، فقال له: لِمَ تَعْبُدُ مَا لَا يَسْمَعُ وَلَا يُبْصِرُ وَلَا يُغْنِي عَنْكَ شَيْئًا؟ ولو انقلب ذلك عليه في معبوده لأضحت حجته داحضة ودلالته ساقطة ولم يصدق قوله تعالى:
The fifth principle: Hearing and Sight. The knowledge that He, exalted, is Hearing, Seeing; nothing of the whisperings of conscience or the hidden thoughts of imagination and reflection escapes His sight, and the sound of the crawling of a black ant on a black rock in a dark night is not hidden from His hearing. And how could He not be Hearing, Seeing, while hearing and sight are undoubtedly perfection, not deficiency? So how can the creature be more perfect than the Creator, the Maker? And the made thing be more sublime and more complete than the Maker? Or how can the division be balanced whenever deficiency occurs in His side and perfection in His creation and handiwork? Or how can the argument of Ibrahim against his father stand, when he was worshipping idols out of ignorance and error, and said to him: 'Why do you worship that which cannot hear, nor see, nor avail you in anything?' And if that were turned back upon Him regarding His worshipped object, His argument would become invalid, His proof unsound, and His statement would not be verified.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth principle of belief: affirming that Allah possesses the attributes of hearing (sam‘) and sight (baṣar). It opens by stating that nothing escapes His sight—not the innermost thoughts or subtle imaginings—and nothing escapes His hearing, even the sound of a black ant crawling on a black rock in a dark night. The author then argues that these attributes are perfections (kamaal) and not deficiencies. Since the Creator is greater than the creature, it would be absurd for the creature to have these perfections while the Creator lacks them. The passage uses the example of the Prophet Ibrahim (peace be upon him) pleading with his father, who worshipped idols that cannot hear or see. If Allah Himself were devoid of hearing and sight, Ibrahim’s argument would be refuted and his proof would collapse. Thus, the attributes of hearing and sight are essential for the true deity and are affirmed for Allah in a manner befitting His majesty, without anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
سَمِيع
بَصِير
كَمَال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Allah's hearing and sight are like human senses requiring physical organs
Correction: Ghazali emphasizes they are perfections free from bodily limitations, befitting Allah's majesty.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali use the argument of Ibrahim (AS) against his father?
A) To show that Ibrahim’s father was wrong to worship idols
B) To prove that if Allah lacked hearing and sight, Ibrahim’s argument would invalidate itself
C) To demonstrate that idols are deaf and blind
D) To compare Allah to idols

Key Takeaway
Allah's attributes of hearing and sight are necessary perfections, affirmed by reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that Allah hears every whisper and sees every hidden thought. What is one secret sin or intention you have been hiding, and will you bring it before Him now in sincere repentance?
?
If hearing and sight are perfections, and you believe in Allah's perfection, how does that change the way you speak or act when you think no one is watching?
?
Ibrahim used the inability of idols to hear or see to prove they were not gods. Do you ever act as if Allah does not see or hear you? When will you truly live in the constant awareness that He sees and hears everything?

The Necessity of Divine Hearing and Sight
Ghazali proves divine hearing and sight as attributes of perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Ghazali argues that God must possess hearing and sight as attributes of perfection, and how this differs from human sensory organs.

Core Idea
Denying God's hearing and sight would make a creature more perfect than the Creator.

This passage from Imam al-Ghazali's Qawa'id al-Aqa'id presents a rational proof for divine hearing and sight. Using rhetorical questions, the argument of Abraham (peace be upon him), and an analogy to other divine attributes, Ghazali establishes that these are necessary attributes of perfection. The goal is to affirm these attributes while maintaining God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the logical flow sink in before analysis.
2
Identify the three main arguments
Notice the three parts: the perfection argument (creature vs Creator), the Abrahamic argument (idols vs God), and the analogy to other attributes (acting without limbs).
These form a cumulative proof.
3
Understand the 'perfection' argument
Why would it be absurd if a creature had a perfection that the Creator lacks? Reflect on the implications.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Connect to divine transcendence
Ghazali emphasizes that God sees without a pupil and hears without an ear. How does this preserve His transcendence?
Important to avoid anthropomorphism.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the main argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكيف يكون المخلوق أكمل من الخالق الصانع ؟ والمصنوع أسنى وأتم من صنعته ؟ أو كيف تعتدل القسمة مهما وقع النقص في جهته والكمال في خلقه ؟ أو كيف تستقيم حجة إبراهيم على أبيه إذ كان يعبد الأصنام جهلاً وغيا فقال له : لِمَ تَعْبُدُ مَا لَا يَسْمَعُ وَلَا يُبْصِرُ وَلَا يُغْنِي عَنْكَ شَيْئاً ؟ ولو انقلب ذلك عليه في معبوده لأضحت حجته داحضة ودلالته ساقطة . وتلك حجتنا آتيناها إبراهيم على قومه . فهو تعالى أحق بالإتصاف بهما من المخلوق . وكما عقل كونه فاعلاً بلا جارحة ، وعالماً بلا قلب ودماغ ، فليعقل كونه بصيراً بلا حدقة ، وسميعاً بلا أذن ، إذ لا فرق بينهما.
So how can the creature be more perfect than the Creator, the Maker? And the manufactured thing more excellent and more complete than its workmanship? Or how can the division be balanced whenever imperfection occurs on His side and perfection on His creation? Or how can the argument of Abraham against his father stand when he worshipped idols out of ignorance and error, saying to him: 'Why do you worship that which does not hear, does not see, and does not avail you anything?' And if that were turned against his deity, his argument would become invalid and his proof would fall. And that is Our argument which We gave to Abraham against his people. So He, exalted, is more deserving of being described with both (hearing and sight) than the creature. And just as it is understood that He is an agent without a limb, and knowing without a heart and brain, so let it be understood that He is seeing without a pupil, and hearing without an ear, for there is no difference between them.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of divine hearing and sight. The author begins by asking rhetorical questions: How can a creature be more perfect than the Creator? If God lacked hearing and sight, then a creature possessing them would be more perfect, which is absurd. He then invokes the argument of Abraham (peace be upon him) against his idol-worshipping father: Abraham criticized idols for lacking hearing and sight. If God Himself lacked these attributes, Abraham's argument would backfire. The author concludes that God is more deserving of these attributes than any creature. Finally, he draws an analogy: just as we accept that God acts without limbs and knows without organs, we must accept that He sees without a pupil and hears without an ear, as there is no difference in principle.

بصر
صفات الكمال
إبراهيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine hearing and sight are similar to human senses involving physical organs.
Correction: Ghazali argues that God hears without an ear and sees without a pupil, emphasizing that these attributes are perfect and beyond physical limitations, unlike human senses.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument for affirming hearing and sight as attributes of God?
A) They are mentioned in the Quran without explanation.
B) Denying them would make a creature more perfect than the Creator.
C) They can be proven by empirical observation.
D) They are necessary for God to communicate with prophets.

Key Takeaway
God must possess hearing and sight as attributes of perfection, but in a manner unlike creation—without organs and beyond human comprehension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
God's hearing and sight are without organs and unlike anything we know. Does that truth comfort you or make you uneasy? Are you willing to submit to a God whose attributes transcend your comprehension, even when you cannot fully grasp them? What does your answer reveal about the depth of your tawheed?

Divine Speech: Eternal Attribute, Not Sound
Ghazali defines divine speech as an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine speech is an eternal attribute of Allah, not composed of sounds or letters, and that true speech is the speech of the soul.

Core Idea
Divine speech is an eternal attribute, not created sounds or letters.

The sixth principle of Islamic creed concerns divine speech. Ghazali immediately clarifies that Allah's speech is not like human speech—it is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence. This passage introduces a key distinction between the inner reality of speech (kalam al-nafs) and the physical sounds and letters that merely point to it. Understanding this distinction is essential for avoiding anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall meaning. Notice the strong negation: 'not sound nor letter'.
The passage uses a comparison with existence to emphasize incomparability.
2
Identify the definition of divine speech
Ghazali states that speech is 'an attribute subsisting in His essence'. Reflect on what this means—an eternal, non-created quality of Allah.
3
Understand the 'speech of the soul' concept
The passage introduces 'kalam al-nafs' as true speech. Sounds and letters are only created indicators. This distinguishes inner meaning from physical expression.
This concept is crucial for later debates on the Qur'an.
4
Apply the principle of incomparability
Just as Allah's existence is unlike any other, His speech is unlike any other. This protects tawhid and avoids likening Allah to creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل السادس : الكلام أنه سبحانه وتعالى متكلم بكلام ، وهو وصف قائم بذاته ، ليس بصوت ولا حرف ، بل لا يشبه كلامه كلام غيره ، كما لا يشبه وجوده وجود غيره ؛ والكلام بالحقيقة كلام النفس ، وإنما الأصوات قطعت حروفاً للدلالات.
The sixth principle: Speech. That He, glorified and exalted, is speaking with speech, and it is an attribute subsisting in His essence, not sound nor letter; rather, His speech does not resemble the speech of others, just as His existence does not resemble the existence of others; and speech in reality is the speech of the soul, while sounds are cut letters for indications.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth principle of Islamic creed concerning divine speech. The author states that God is speaking (mutakallim) with a speech that is an attribute subsisting in His essence. This speech is not composed of sounds or letters, which are accidental and created. Rather, it is an eternal attribute. The author emphasizes that God's speech is incomparable to human speech, just as His existence is incomparable. Then he explains that true speech is 'the speech of the soul' (kalam al-nafs), i.e., the inner meaning, while the sounds and letters we utter are merely created signs that indicate that inner speech.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine speech is composed of sounds and letters like human speech.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that divine speech is an eternal attribute without sound or letter; sounds and letters are created signs that indicate inner speech.

Checkpoint

A) Sounds and letters.
B) The speech of the soul (kalam al-nafs).
C) The words revealed in scripture.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is an eternal attribute of Allah, entirely unlike created speech, and true speech is the inner meaning (kalam al-nafs), not physical sounds or letters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage says Allah's speech does not resemble any other speech. Does your reverence for the Qur'an reflect this uniqueness, or do you approach it like any other book? What will you change starting today?

The Hearing-Seeing Analogy for Divine Speech
Ghazali uses the analogy of seeing God to defend hearing speech without sound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that if we accept seeing God in the afterlife without a body or color, we must also accept hearing divine speech without sound or letter.

Core Idea
If you accept seeing God without form, accept hearing His speech without sound.

Ghazali begins by addressing someone who finds it impossible that Moses heard speech without sound or letters. He cleverly turns the tables: if you believe you will see God in the afterlife without a body or color—something you have never experienced—then why not also accept hearing speech without sound? This analogy trains the mind to accept divine realities beyond physical categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening challenge
Notice how Ghazali frames the objection: someone finds it unlikely that Moses heard speech without sound or letter. He calls that person 'without a guide'.
The phrase 'لا هادي له' indicates a strong criticism.
2
Identify the parallel argument
Ghazali compares hearing speech without sound to seeing God without body or color. Both are accepted in Islamic theology.
Seeing God in the afterlife is a Sunni doctrine, so the analogy uses a shared belief.
3
Understand the logical move
If you already accept one miraculous perception (sight of the non-physical), you should accept another (hearing the non-physical).
4
Reflect on the implication
This shows that divine speech is not like human speech; it is an attribute of God, not a physical phenomenon.
This connects to the broader lesson on divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هاد (۱) ، ومن استبعد أن يسمع موسى عليه السلام في الدنيا كلاماً ليس بصوت ولا حرف فليستنكر أن يرى في الآخرة موجوداً ليس بجسم ولا لون (۲) وإن عقل أن يرى ما ليس بلون ولا جسم ولا قدر ولا كمية ، وهو إلى الآن لم ير غيره فليعقل في حاسة السمع ما عقله في حاسة البصر (۳) وإن عقل أن يكون له علم واحد هو علم بجميع الموجودات ، فليعقل صفة واحدة للذات هو كلام بجميع ما دل عليه بالعبارات(٤) ؛ وإن عقل كون السموات السبع وكون الجنة والنار مكتوبة في ورقة صغيرة ومحفوظة في مقدار(ه) ذرة من القلب وأن كل ذلك مرئي في مقدار عدسة الحدقة من من غير أن تحل ذات ـ السموات والارض والجنة والنار في الحدقة والقلب والورقة ، فليعقل كون الكلام مقروءاً بالألسنة ، محفوظا في القلوب ، مكتوبا في المصاحف(٦) من غير حلول ذات الكلام فيها ، إذ لو حلت بكتاب الله ذات الكلام في الورق(٧) لحل (۱) ليس له من هادٍ يرشده إلى سلوك سبيل السداد (۲) ولا قابل للحوادث ، وأن المقصود نفي الكيفية على كل حال ، وكذلك إذا إستبعدوا كيف سمع جبريل عليه السلام والمؤمنون غداً كيف يسمعون ؟ فالجواب سمع كلاماً ليس بحرف ولا صوت من متكلم حتى ليس له لسان وشفه ؛ (۳) يعني فليعقل سماع ماليس بصوت ، وهو لا يكون إلا بطريق خرق العادة كما نبه عليه الباقلاني (٤) من أمر ونهى وأخبار ، وقد جاز في الشاهد أن يكون الشيء الواحد أمراً ونهياً وخبراً وإستخباراً فكذلك يجوز في الغائب ولم يكن مستحيلاً ، وهذه العبارات مخلوقة لأنها أصوات وهي أعراض سميت تلك العبارات كلام الله لدلالتها عليه وتأديه بها ، والإختلاف في العبارات المؤدية لا الكلام (٥) وفي نسخة أخرى : في تعداد ذرة من القلب ... الخ (٦) وفي نسخة أخرى : مكتوباً في المصاحف بالأحبار المتنوعة من غير حلول ذات الكلام فيها (۷) كلمة ( في الورق ) لم توجد في النسخ الأخرى ١٨٤
A guide (1). And whoever finds it unlikely that Moses (peace be upon him) heard in this world speech that is not a sound nor a letter, then let him find it unlikely that he will see in the afterlife an existent that is not a body nor a color (2). And if he understands that he sees what is not a color, not a body, not a dimension, and not a quantity, and he until now has seen nothing else, then let him understand in the sense of hearing what he understood in the sense of sight (3). And if he understands that He has one knowledge which is knowledge of all existents, then let him understand one attribute of the essence which is speech comprising all that is indicated by expressions (4). And if he understands that the seven heavens, Paradise, and Hellfire are written in a small sheet and preserved in the amount (5) of a speck from the heart, and that all of that is visible in the amount of a lens of the pupil without the essence of the heavens, earth, Paradise, and Hellfire entering into the pupil, heart, and sheet, then let him understand that speech is read by tongues, preserved in hearts, and written in manuscripts (6) without the essence of speech entering into them. For if the essence of speech had entered into the Book of God in the paper (7), it would have entered. (1) He has no guide to show him the path of rectitude. (2) And not receptive to accidents, and the intent is the negation of modality in any case; similarly, if they find it unlikely how Gabriel heard and the believers tomorrow will hear, the answer is that he heard speech not a letter nor a sound from a speaker who has no tongue nor lip. (3) Meaning: let him understand hearing what is not a sound, and that only occurs by way of a break in custom, as al-Baqillani indicated. (4) Of command, prohibition, and reports; and it is permissible in the empirical world that one thing be a command, a prohibition, a report, and an interrogation. So likewise it is permissible in the unseen and is not impossible. And these expressions are created because they are sounds and accidents; those expressions are called the speech of God because they indicate and communicate it, and the difference is in the expressions that communicate, not in the speech. (5) And in another copy: 'in the count of a speck from the heart' etc. (6) And in another copy: 'written in the manuscripts with diverse inks without the essence of speech entering into them.' (7) The word 'in the paper' is not found in other copies. 184
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents analogical arguments to demonstrate that God's speech is not composed of letters or sounds. It opens by addressing someone who finds it unlikely that Moses heard speech without sound or letter; the author argues that such a person should equally find it unlikely that God will be seen in the afterlife without body or color, yet the latter is accepted by Muslims. Using parallelism, the author says: if one can conceive of seeing something without body or color (i.e., God in the afterlife), then one should also conceive of hearing something without sound (i.e., divine speech). Next, the author uses the analogy of God's knowledge: just as God has one knowledge that encompasses all things, He has one speech attribute that commands, prohibits, reports, and interrogates all at once. This is not impossible, since in the observable world, one statement can have multiple functions. Then, a third analogy: if one accepts that the vast heavens, Paradise, and Hellfire can be written on a small sheet of paper and seen in a tiny pupil without physically entering those mediums, then one should also accept that the Quran is read by tongues, preserved in hearts, and written in manuscripts without God's literal speech entering them. The footnote (4) clarifies that these expressions are created sounds and accidents, called 'God's speech' because they indicate and convey it, and the variation is in the expressions, not the eternal speech. Footnote (6) reiterates that the essence of speech does not enter the manuscripts.

Key Terms
سماع
بصر
كلام
جسم
لون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's speech must be audible like human speech, with letters and sounds.
Correction: Ghazali shows that just as God is seen without body or color, His speech is heard without sound or letter.

Checkpoint
What famous unseen event does Ghazali use to parallel hearing speech without sound?
A) Creation of the heavens
B) Seeing God in the afterlife
C) Resurrection of the dead
D) Splitting of the moon

Key Takeaway
Acceptance of one miracle (seeing God) logically requires acceptance of another (hearing His speech without sound).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that you will see Allah in the afterlife without form or color. Why then do you struggle to accept that He spoke to Moses without sound? What does this reveal about your concept of God?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you ever feel that you are hearing Allah's speech without sound? How can this lesson reshape your relationship with the divine word?
?
Have you ever rejected a theological truth simply because it didn't fit your sensory experience? How does this analogy call you to expand your understanding of Allah's attributes?

Further Analogies: One Speech and Writing on a Small Sheet
Ghazali presents analogies from God's knowledge and from writing to defend non-corporeal divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two additional analogies showing that divine speech can be one yet contain commands, prohibitions, etc., and can be written in books without physically entering them.

Core Idea
One divine speech encompasses all, just as a small sheet holds vast worlds.

Ghazali continues with two more powerful analogies. First, just as God has one all-encompassing knowledge, He has one speech that includes every command and prohibition. Second, if you can accept that the entire heavens and hell can be written on a tiny sheet and seen in a small pupil without physically entering, then you can accept that the Quran can be recited by tongues, memorized in hearts, and written in manuscripts without God's actual speech residing there. These analogies reinforce the non-material nature of divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the knowledge analogy
Ghazali says: if you understand that God has one knowledge that encompasses all existents, then understand that He has one speech that encompasses all commands and reports.
This shows divine speech is not multiple but a single attribute.
2
Notice the expressions
The expressions (words) are created and indicate the eternal speech. They are called 'speech of God' because they convey it.
The difference is in the expressions, not the speech itself.
3
Read the writing analogy
If you accept that huge things (heavens, hell) are written on a small sheet and seen in a tiny pupil without entering them, then accept that the Quran is written in books without God's speech entering the paper.
This refutes physicalist interpretations of the Quran.
4
Understand the conclusion
Divine speech does not reside in created things; it is an attribute of God that is expressed through created words.

The Eternity of Divine Speech
Ghazali establishes the eternity of divine speech and begins a trilemma refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the doctrine that Allah's speech is eternal and the initial step of the proof that it cannot be temporal due to the impossibility of temporal events inhering in Allah's essence.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is eternal; temporal speech would require inherence in His essence, which is impossible.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali establishes a core Sunni doctrine: that divine speech is an eternal attribute of Allah, not a created thing. He identifies several sects that consider it temporal, then presents a trilemma to refute them. Even though the argument is incomplete in this excerpt, the first logical impossibility is clear: temporal events cannot subsist in Allah's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once aloud, then read the literal translation. Notice how Ghazali begins with a clear statement of belief before addressing opposing views.
The Arabic uses technical terms like 'قدم' (eternity) and 'حادث' (temporal event).
2
Identify the main claim and the sects
Find the sentence that declares the speech and attributes are eternal. Then list the five sects that oppose this: Mu'tazila, Najjariyya, Zaydiyya, Imamiyya, Khawarij.
Some of these sects avoided saying 'created' while the later Mu'tazila explicitly said so.
3
Understand the trilemma
The author says that if speech were temporal, it would have to subsist in one of three places: in Allah's essence, in a body, or not in a locus. The first option is impossible. Write down why it is impossible according to the passage.
The passage explicitly says: 'temporal events cannot subsist in the Essence of the Creator.'
4
Reflect on the unfinished argument
The passage stops after rejecting the first option. Based on the logic given, think about why the other two options would also be impossible. This will help you appreciate the full argument.
This reflection prepares you for the continuation of the proof in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل السابع : قدم الكلام والصفات والتنزه عن حلول الحوادث : أعلم أن الكلام القائم بنفسه قديم ، وكذا جميع صفاته ، إذ يستحيل أن يكون محلاً للحوادث داخلاً تحت التغير ، بل يجب للصفات من نعوت القدم ما يجب للذات فلا تعتريه التغيرات ، ولا تحله الحادثات بل لم يزل في قدمه موصوفاً بمحامد الصفات ، ولا يزال في أبده كذلك منزهاً عن تغير الحالات وذهبت المعتزلة والنجارية والزيدية ، والإمامية ، والخوارج إلى أن كلام الله حادث . وامتنع طائفة من هؤلاء من إطلاق القول بكونه مخلوقاً وأطلق المتؤخرون من المعتزلة كونه مخلوقاً . وسموه ونحن نقول : لو كان كلام الله حادثاً لم يخل من أمور ثلاثة: إما أن يقوم بذات الباري ، أو بجسم من الأجسام، أو لا بمحل باطل قيامه به ، فإن الحوادث يستحيل قيامها بذات الباري سبحانه وتعالى
The seventh principle: The eternity of speech and attributes, and transcendence over the inherence of temporal events. Know that speech subsisting in itself is eternal (qadim), and likewise all of His attributes, for it is impossible that He be a locus for temporal events, subject to change. Rather, the attributes must have the same characteristics of eternity that the Essence must have, so changes do not befall them, nor do temporal events inhere in them. Rather, He has never ceased in His eternity to be described with praiseworthy attributes, and He will never cease in His perpetuity to be likewise, transcendent over the change of states. The Mu'tazila, Najjariyya, Zaydiyya, Imamiyya, and Khawarij held that Allah's speech is temporal (hadith). A group among them refrained from explicitly calling it created, while the later Mu'tazila called it created. And they named it... And we say: If Allah's speech were temporal, it could not be free from three possibilities: either it subsists in the Essence of the Creator, or in a body among bodies, or not in a locus. The first is invalid because temporal events cannot subsist in the Essence of the Creator, exalted and high.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement and a proof (istidlal) for the eternity of divine speech. The author begins with the principle: the speech that subsists (qaim bi dhatihi) in Allah's essence is eternal, along with all attributes. The reason is that it is impossible for Allah to be a locus (mahall) for temporal events (hawadith) or subject to change. Attributes must share the same eternal properties as the essence: they are not subject to change, nor do temporal events inhere in them. Allah has always been described with perfect attributes and always will be, free from changing states. Then the author lists the sects (Mu'tazila, Najjariyya, Zaydiyya, Imamiyya, Khawarij) who held that Allah's speech is temporal (hadith). Some of them avoided calling it 'created' (makhluq), but later Mu'tazila explicitly said it is created. The author then presents a demonstration: if speech were temporal, it would have to subsist either (1) in Allah's essence, or (2) in a body, or (3) not in any locus. The first is impossible because temporal events cannot subsist in Allah's essence (since that would imply change and composition). The passage ends here, but the argument implies the remaining two possibilities are also impossible, thus speech cannot be temporal.

Key Terms
قدم
كلام
صفات
قديم
حادث
حلول الحوادث
محل
مخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'eternal speech' refers to a physical sound or temporal utterance that has always existed as a primordial sound.
Correction: The passage clarifies that eternal speech is an attribute subsisting in Allah's essence, not a created sound or literal words. It is not subject to change or time.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for divine speech to be temporal (حادث)?
A) Because the Mu'tazila say so.
B) Because if it were temporal, it would have to subsist in Allah's essence, which is impossible for temporal events.
C) Because it is written in the Quran.
D) Because it was revealed to the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech is an eternal attribute, not a temporal creation, because temporal events cannot inhere in His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ghazali argues that temporal events cannot inhere in Allah. Consider your own life: when you sin and then repent, do you ever imagine that your actions cause a change in Allah's will or speech? How does this passage correct that assumption, and what does it demand of you in terms of your understanding of Allah's constancy?
?
The passage lists sects that considered divine speech temporal. Reflect on your own heart: are there any commands or prohibitions in the Quran that you secretly wish were 'temporal'—subject to change or not applicable to you? How does the eternity of Allah's speech confront that wish, and what change in attitude is required of you?

The Seventh Principle: Pre-Eternality of Divine Speech and Attributes
Ghazali states the principle that God's speech and attributes are pre-eternal and unchanging.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's speech and attributes must be pre-eternal and unchanging according to Ash'ari theology.

Core Idea
God's timeless perfection means His attributes are eternal, never created or changed.

This principle tackles a fundamental question: does God's speech—and by extension His attributes—have a beginning? Ghazali asserts no: they are eternal just as God is eternal. In this passage, you'll see the rational proof from God's impossibility to be a locus for temporal events. This sets the stage for the later debate on the Quran's nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the key terms: قدم, كلام, صفات, حوادث.
2
Identify the core argument
The passage argues that God cannot be a locus for temporal events. Identify this phrase and understand why it leads to the conclusion that His attributes are eternal.
The impossibility of change in God is central.
3
Understand the distinction
Notice that Ghazali speaks of 'the speech that subsists in His essence' (الكلام القائم بنفسه). This is the eternal speech, distinguished from the created expressions. Reflect on how this relates to the Quran.
This distinction is key to later debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل السابع : قدم الكلام والصفات والتنزه عن حلول الحوادث : أعلم أن الكلام القائم بنفسه قديم ، وكذا جميع صفاته ، إذ يستحيل أن يكون محلاً للحوادث داخلاً تحت التغير ، بل يجب للصفات من نعوت القدم ما يجب للذات فلا تعتريه التغيرات ، ولا تحله الحادثات بل لم يزل في قدمه موصوفاً بمحامد الصفات ، ولا يزال في أبده كذلك منزهاً عن تغير الحالات
The seventh principle: The pre-eternality of speech and attributes, and being transcendent above the occurrence of temporal events. Know that the speech that subsists in His essence is pre-eternal, as are all His attributes, since it is impossible for Him to be a locus for temporal events or subject to change. Rather, the attributes of pre-eternality are incumbent upon the attributes just as they are incumbent upon the essence, so changes do not affect them, and temporal events do not inhere in them. Rather, He has never ceased in His pre-eternality to be described with praiseworthy attributes, and He will never cease in His perpetuity to be thus, transcendent above the change of states.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage defines the seventh principle of Islamic creed according to the Ash'ari school: God's speech (and all attributes) are pre-eternal, not created. Ghazali argues by impossibility: if God were a locus for temporal events (ḥawādith), He would be subject to change, which is impossible for the eternal. Therefore, His attributes must be eternal like His essence. The phrase 'الكلام القائم بنفسه' refers to the eternal speech subsisting in God's essence, distinguished from the created expressions (sounds and letters). This principle sets the foundation for the debate on whether the Quran is created.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran as recited is eternal and uncreated in its entirety.
Correction: According to Ghazali, the eternal speech is the attribute of God; the recited words, letters, and sounds are created expressions of that eternal speech.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is written in the Quran
B) Because God cannot be a locus for temporal events
C) Because prophets heard it
D) Because the Quran is uncreated

Key Takeaway
God's attributes, including His speech, are eternal and unchanging because He cannot be subject to temporal events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ghazali says God has never ceased to be described with praiseworthy attributes. When you look at your own life, do you often act as if God’s goodness or approval changes based on your performance? How can you anchor your sense of worth in His unchanging attributes rather than your shifting actions?
?
The speech of God is eternal and subsists in His essence. Yet, how often do you read the Quran as if it were just a book of historical guidance rather than the very attribute of God addressed to you? What would it practically mean for your next recitation to treat it as the eternal voice of God speaking to you now?

Refuting Created Speech: The Trilemma
Ghazali enumerates three impossible scenarios for created speech and concludes it must be eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's trilemma against created speech and why divine speech must be an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence.

Core Idea
Created speech cannot exist in God, a body, or nowhere—so it must be eternal.

Ghazali addresses a major controversy: Is the Quran created? He refutes the Mu'tazilite claim by showing that a created speech would have to subsist either in God, a body, or without a locus—all impossible. This lesson walks through his argument and the analogy that follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sects holding created speech
Read the opening lines to see which groups Ghazali lists as proponents of created speech. Note that some hesitated to call it 'created' while later Mu'tazila did.
This sets the stage for the refutation.
2
Understand the three possibilities
Ghazali presents a trilemma: if speech were created, it must either subsist in God's essence, in a body, or without any locus. Memorize these three.
3
Examine why each possibility is impossible
Follow Ghazali's reasoning for each: (1) God cannot be a locus for temporal events, (2) if in a body, that body would be the speaker, (3) an accident cannot subsist by itself.
This is the core of the argument.
4
See the conclusion and analogy
Ghazali concludes that speech subsists in God's essence and the temporal aspect is only the sounds. Then he illustrates with the analogy of a father's eternal request and the son's later hearing.
The analogy helps grasp how eternal speech can be heard in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبت المعتزلة والنجارية والزيدية ، والإمامية ، والخوارج إلى أن كلام الله حادث . وامتنع طائفة من هؤلاء من إطلاق القول بكونه مخلوقاً وأطلق المتؤخرون من المعتزلة كونه مخلوقاً . وسموه ونحن نقول : لو كان كلام الله حادثاً لم يخل من أمور ثلاثة : حادثاً إما أن يقوم بذات الباري ، أو بجسم من الأجسام، أو لا بمحل باطل قيامه به ، فإن الحوادث يستحيل قيامها بذات الباري سبحانه وتعالى
لأن ما كان محل الحوادث لا يخلو عنها وما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث (١) وإنما ثبت نعت الحدوث للأجسام من حيث تعرضها للتغير وتقلب الأوصاف ، فكيف يكون خالقها مشاركاً لها في قبول التغير وينبني على هذا أن كلامه قائم بذاته ، وإنما الحادث هي الأصوات الدالة عليه (٢) وكما عقل قيام طلب التعلم وارادته بذات الوالد للولد قبل أن يخلق ولده حتى إذا خلق ولده وعقل وخلق الله له علمًا متعلقاً بما في قلب أبيه من الطلب، صار مأمورا بذلك الطلب الذي قام بذات أبيه ودام وجوده إلى وقت معرفة ولده (٣) له، فليعقل قيام الطلب الذي دل عليه قوله عز وجل : اخْلَعْ نَعْلَيْكَ (٤) بذات الله ، ومصير موسى عليه السلام مخاطبا به بعد وجوده ، إذ خلقت له معرفة بذلك الطلب ، وسمع لذلك الكلام القديم (٥)
(١) لأنه لا تقوم الحوادث إلا بحادث ، ولو قام بجسم لكان المتكلم ذلك الجسم ويبطل وجود الكلام لا في محل ، لإنه عرض من الأعراض ، ويستحيل قيام الأعراض بأنفسها إذ لو جاز ذلك في ضرب منها لجاز في سائرها
(٢) ولتعلم أن القرآن بالمعنى الأزلي لا يدخل تحت الزمان ، ولا يوصف بماض ولا مستقبل ولا حال ضرورة أن الأزلي مناف للزمان لأن الزمان من لواحق الحادث ، ولا شيء من الحوادث بأزلي وأما بمعنى الفعل الدال على ذلك أو بعض ما هو متعلق ذلك فنعم .
(٣) وفي نسخة أخرى : ودام وجوده إلى وقت معرفة إبنه
(٤) الآية : ١٢ من سورة طه
(٥) سمع يتعدى باللام تارة كما جرى عليه المصنف ، ومثله : سمع الله لمن حمده . ويتعدى أيضاً بلا لام أخرى، ومنه قوله : قد سمع الله قول التي تجادلك في زوجها ، وهذا قول الأشعري وأنكر الماتريدي : سماعه الكلام النفسي ، وعنده أنه سمع صوتاً دالا على كلام الله تعالى
The Mu'tazila, Najjariyya, Zaydiyya, Imamiyya, and Khawarij held that God's speech is created. A group among them refrained from saying it is created, but the later Mu'tazila explicitly called it created. And we say: If God's speech were created, it would not be free from three matters: either it subsists in God's essence, or in a body, or without a locus. That it subsists in God's essence is false, for temporal events are impossible to subsist in God Almighty. Because whatever is a locus for temporal events cannot be free from them, and whatever is not free from temporal events is itself created (1). And the attribute of being created is established for bodies due to their being subject to change and variation of attributes. So how can their Creator share with them in accepting change? And based on this, His speech subsists in His essence, and the temporal events are only the sounds that indicate it (2). And just as one rationally grasps the subsistence of a request for learning and its will in the essence of a father for his son before creating his son, until when he created his son and he understood, and Allah created for him knowledge pertaining to what was in his father's heart of the request, he became commanded by that request which subsisted in his father's essence and continued to exist until the time of his son's recognition of him (3), so let one rationally grasp the subsistence of the request indicated by His words 'Take off your sandals' (4) in God's essence, and that Moses, peace be upon him, became addressed by it after his existence, when knowledge of that request was created for him, and he heard that pre-eternal speech (5). (1) Because temporal events do not occur except in something created, and if it subsisted in a body, that body would be the speaker, and the existence of speech without a locus would be invalid, for it is an accident, and it is impossible for accidents to subsist by themselves, for if that were allowed in one type, it would be allowed in all. (2) And know that the Qur'an in the eternal sense does not enter under time, nor is it described by past, future, or present, necessarily because the eternal is incompatible with time, because time is an accident of the temporal, and none of the temporal things is eternal. As for in the sense of the act indicating that or some of what pertains to it, then yes. (3) In another copy: 'and its existence continued until the time of his son's recognition'. (4) Verse 12 of Surah Ta-Ha. (5) 'Heard' is transitive with the preposition 'lam' sometimes, as the author used it, and similarly 'Allah hears the one who praises Him'. It also is transitive without 'lam' in other cases, such as 'Indeed Allah has heard the statement of the woman who argues with you concerning her husband'. This is the position of al-Ash'ari. Al-Maturidi denied hearing the internal speech, and according to him, he heard a sound indicating God's speech.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that God's speech is created. Ghazali first lists the sects holding this view (Mu'tazila, Najjariyya, Zaydiyya, Imamiyya, Khawarij) and notes that some hesitated to call it 'created' while later Mu'tazila explicitly did. He then presents a trilemma: if speech were created, it must either (1) subsist in God's essence, (2) subsist in a body, or (3) subsist without any locus. He argues each is impossible. (1) God cannot be a locus for temporal events because whatever is a locus for temporal events is itself subject to change and therefore created—God is the Creator, not subject to such. (2) If it subsisted in a body, that body would be the speaker, not God. (3) It cannot subsist without a locus because it is an accident, and accidents require a substrate; otherwise, all accidents would be self-subsistent, which is absurd. From this, Ghazali concludes that God's speech must subsist in His essence (eternal attribute), and the temporal aspect is only the sounds that indicate it. He then provides an analogy: a father's eternal request (like 'learn') subsists in his essence before the son is created; when the son is created and given knowledge of that request, he becomes obligated and the request is 'heard'. Similarly, God's eternal command 'Take off your sandals' existed in His essence, and when Moses existed and was given knowledge of it, he heard it. The footnotes clarify that the eternal Qur'an is not subject to time (contrary to created expressions), and that al-Ash'ari held that Moses heard the eternal speech, while al-Maturidi held he heard only a created sound indicating it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran is entirely created, including its meaning.
Correction: Ghazali distinguishes between the eternal attribute (meaning) which subsists in God's essence, and the created sounds and letters which are temporal indicators.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three possibilities for created speech that Ghazali refutes?
A) It subsists in God's essence
B) It subsists in a body
C) It subsists without a locus
D) It subsists in the Preserved Tablet

Key Takeaway
God's speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence; only the sounds and letters that express it are created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You recite the Qur'an daily. Does it give you a sense of connection to an eternal attribute of God, or do you treat it as just another book? How should your reverence change knowing that these words are not created but subsist in Allah's essence?
?
The analogy of a father's request before the son's existence suggests that God's command to you existed before you were born. How does that truth impact your sense of accountability for obeying His commands?
?
If you believe that the Qur'an is created, you are implicitly attributing change to God. Do you realize the seriousness of such a belief? Examine your own heart: Do you ever think of God as 'changing His mind' or 'revising' His will? How can this lesson correct that?

Bodies Are Originated
Ghazali states the principle that whatever is a locus of accidents is originated and applies it to bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that bodies are originated because they cannot be free from accidents.

Core Idea
Whatever is a locus of accidents is originated, and bodies are such loci.

Before we can affirm that God's speech is pre-eternal, we must first recognize that everything in creation—especially bodies—is originated. Ghazali begins with a foundational principle: whatever serves as a place for accidents cannot be free from them, and whatever is never free from accidents is itself originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the logical flow: premise, reason, conclusion.
The argument is concise; each word carries weight.
2
Identify the key principle
Find the sentence that states the general rule: 'what is a locus of accidents cannot be free from them, and whatever is not free from accidents is originated.'
This is the core premise for proving that bodies are originated.
3
Apply the principle to bodies
Notice how Ghazali applies the principle: bodies are subject to change and alteration of attributes, so they are loci of accidents and therefore originated.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: if bodies are originated, what does that say about the Creator? The passage hints that the Creator cannot share in this attribute of change.
This sets up the next lesson on God's speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۱) لأنه لا تقوم الحوادث إلا بحادث ، ولو قام بجسم لكان المتكلم ذلك الجسم ويبطل وجود الكلام لا في محل ، لإنه عرض من الأعراض ، ويستحيل قيام الأعراض بأنفسها إذ لو جاز ذلك في ضرب منها لجاز في سائرها

(۳) وفي نسخة أخرى : ودام وجوده إلى وقت معرفة إبنه . الخ
(٤) الآية : ١٢ من سورة طه
(٥) سمع يتعدى باللام تارة كما جرى عليه المصنف ، ومثله : سمع الله لمن حمده . ويتعدى أيضاً بلا لام أخرى، ومنه قوله : قد سمع الله قول التي تجادلك في زوجها ، وهذا قول الأشعري وأنكر الماتريدي : سماعه الكلام النفسي ، وعنده أنه سمع صوتاً دالا على كلام الله تعالى
Because what is a locus of accidents cannot be free from them, and whatever is not free from accidents is originated (ḥādith) (1). Rather, the attribute of origination is affirmed for bodies by virtue of their being subject to change and the alteration of attributes. How then could their Creator share with them in accepting change? Upon this is built that His speech subsists in His essence (qā'im bi dhātihī), and only the sounds indicating it are originated (2). Just as one understands the subsistence of the request for learning and its will in the essence of a father for his son before he creates his son, then when his son is created and understands, and Allah creates for him knowledge relating to what is in his father’s heart of the request, he becomes commanded by that request which subsisted in his father’s essence and continued to exist until the time of his son’s knowledge of it (3), so let one understand the subsistence of the request indicated by His saying, Mighty and Majestic, 'Take off your sandals' (4) in Allah’s essence, and the situation of Moses, peace be upon him, as addressed by it after his existence, when knowledge of that request was created for him, and he heard that pre-eternal speech (5).

(1) Because accidents only subsist in an originated [entity], and if speech subsisted in a body, that body would be the speaker, and the existence of speech without a locus would be invalid, for it is an accident (ʿaraḍ), and it is impossible for accidents to subsist by themselves, for if that were permissible for one kind of them, it would be permissible for all.
(2) And know that the Qur’an in the pre-eternal sense does not enter under time, and is not described by past, future, or present, necessarily because the pre-eternal is opposed to time, for time is an accident of the originated, and none of the originated things is pre-eternal. As for the meaning of the act indicating that, or some of what pertains to it, then yes [it is temporal].
(3) In another copy: and continued to exist until the time of his son’s knowledge.
(4) The verse: 12 of Surah Ṭā Hā.
(5) 'He heard' (samiʿa) is transitive with the lām sometimes, as the author has used it, and similarly 'Allah hears the one who praises Him.' It is also transitive without lām at other times, as in His saying, 'Allah has heard the speech of the one who argues with you concerning her husband.' This is the position of al-Ashʿarī. Al-Māturīdī denied hearing the inner speech (al-kalām al-nafsī), and according to him he heard a sound indicating Allah’s speech.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage contains a complex argument that serves as a proof for the pre-eternity of God’s speech (kalām qadīm) and the origination of bodies. It begins by stating a general principle: whatever is a locus of accidents (maḥall al-ḥawādith) cannot be free from them, and whatever is not free from accidents is itself originated (ḥādith). This premise is used to prove that bodies are originated because they undergo change and alteration of attributes. The author then asks rhetorically how the Creator could share with His creation in accepting change, thus establishing that God is not a body and does not change. From this it follows that His speech subsists in His essence (qā'im bi dhātihī) and is not subject to change; only the sounds indicating it are originated. The author supports this with an analogy: a father’s command to his child to learn exists in the father’s essence before the child is even created. When the child is later created and given knowledge of that command, he becomes obligated by it. Similarly, the command 'Take off your sandals' subsisted in God’s essence, and Moses, upon his existence, was created with knowledge of that command and heard the pre-eternal speech. The footnotes clarify key points: (1) explains that accidents cannot subsist without a body, and if speech were an accident subsisting in a body, that body would be the speaker; (2) distinguishes between the pre-eternal Qur’an and the temporal act; (5) notes the difference between Ashʿarī and Māturīdī on whether the inner speech is heard.

جسم
عرض

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think bodies are eternal or uncreated.
Correction: This passage proves bodies are originated because they constantly undergo change and are never free from accidents.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the reason why bodies are considered originated?
A) Because they are made of atoms.
B) Because they are loci of accidents and are never free from them.
C) Because they were created by God.
D) Because they have color.

Key Takeaway
Bodies are originated because they are always accompanied by changing accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are constantly changing—your age, health, thoughts, and circumstances. If this change proves you are originated, how does that affect your understanding of your own beginning and your ultimate dependence on the Unchanging Creator?
?
The passage says bodies cannot be free from accidents. You have witnessed countless changes in your own body and soul. Do you truly live as if your entire existence is contingent and originated, or do you sometimes act as if you are self-sufficient?
?
If God, being unoriginated, does not change, then what about your desire for change in your life? How can you seek stability and transformation in a way that aligns with your originated nature while relying on the Unchangeable?

God's Speech Is Pre-Eternal
Ghazali uses an analogy to demonstrate that God's speech is pre-eternal and subsisting in His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God's speech is pre-eternal and not subject to change, using the analogy of a father's command.

Core Idea
God's speech subsists in His essence, not in a body, so it is not originated.

Having established that bodies are originated, Ghazali now addresses the nature of God's speech. Since God is not a body, His speech cannot be an accident in a body. Instead, it subsists in His essence and is pre-eternal. The analogy of a father's command helps illustrate how a command can exist before it is heard.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage, then the translation. Note the transition from the earlier argument about bodies to this new section about speech.
The phrase 'upon this is built' connects the two parts.
2
Understand the analogy
Follow the analogy carefully: a father has a desire for his son to learn before the son exists. That desire subsists in the father's essence. When the son is created and given knowledge of that desire, he becomes commanded.
This parallels God's command to Moses existing in His essence before Moses was created.
3
Apply the analogy to God's speech
Ghazali says: similarly, the command 'Take off your sandals' subsisted in God's essence, and Moses was created with knowledge of it and heard the pre-eternal speech.
The hearing of pre-eternal speech is discussed in footnote 5.
4
Consider the implications
This shows that God's speech is not created; it is an eternal attribute. The physical sounds are created, but the speech itself is not.
The footnote also notes a difference between Ash'ari and Maturidi on hearing inner speech.

This passage contains a complex argument that serves as a proof for the pre-eternity of God’s speech (kalām qadīm) and the origination of bodies. It begins by stating a general principle: whatever is a locus of accidents (maḥall al-ḥawādith) cannot be free from them, and whatever is not free from accidents is itself originated (ḥādith). This premise is used to prove that bodies are originated because they undergo change and alteration of attributes. The author then asks rhetorically how the Creator could share with His creation in accepting change, thus establishing that God is not a body and does not change. From this it follows that His speech subsists in His essence (qā'im bi dhātihī) and is not subject to change; only the sounds indicating it are originated. The author supports this with an analogy: a father’s command to his child to learn exists in the father’s essence before the child is even created. When the child is later created and given knowledge of that command, he becomes obligated by it. Similarly, the command 'Take off your sandals' subsisted in God’s essence, and Moses, upon his existence, was created with knowledge of that command and heard the pre-eternal speech. The footnotes clarify key points: (1) explains that accidents cannot subsist without a body, and if speech were an accident subsisting in a body, that body would be the speaker; (2) distinguishes between the pre-eternal Qur’an and the temporal act; (5) notes the difference between Ashʿarī and Māturīdī on whether the inner speech is heard.

Key Terms
كلام قديم
حادث
ذات
سماع

Misconception: Some think that God's speech (the Qur'an) is created in time like human speech.
Correction: This passage shows that God's speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence; only the physical sounds indicating it are created.

Checkpoint

B) The pre-eternal speech of God.
C) The angel Gabriel.
D) The physical book of the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
God's speech is pre-eternal, subsisting in His essence; only the sounds indicating it are created.

?
God's command for Moses existed before Moses himself. Your own purpose and obligations were known to God before you were born. How does this truth affect the way you respond to God's commands today?
?
Do you ever find yourself reciting the Qur'an mechanically, forgetting it is the pre-eternal speech of the Creator? What specific step will you take this week to listen to or recite the Qur'an with deeper awe?

The Pre-Eternity of Divine Knowledge
Ghazali defines the pre-eternity of God's knowledge and refutes originated attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's knowledge is pre-eternal and unchanging, and why new knowledge cannot occur in God.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is eternal and does not change when events occur.

Ghazali now turns to the attribute of knowledge, establishing that God's knowledge is pre-eternal (qadīm) and does not increase or change with the occurrence of created things. This principle is crucial because it safeguards God's perfection and immutability. The lesson uses a clear analogy and logical arguments to demonstrate why God's knowledge cannot be originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the first part of the passage
Read the opening lines where Ghazali states that God's knowledge is pre-eternal and encompasses His essence, attributes, and creations.
Notice the immediate denial of new knowledge arising.
2
Study the analogy of Zayd's arrival
Examine the analogy: if we have persistent knowledge of a future event, we don't need new knowledge when it occurs. This illustrates how God's eternal knowledge suffices.
The analogy is for human understanding; God's knowledge is not created.
3
Explore the arguments against originated attributes
Read the footnote arguments (1) showing that God cannot be described by originated things because that would imply deficiency or change.
These are kalam proofs.
4
Reflect on the implication of pre-eternity
Consider the conclusion that pre-eternal knowledge cannot cease, and that changes apply only to known objects, not to knowledge itself.
This reinforces the doctrine of divine immutability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثامن : قدم العلم
أن علمه قديم ، فلم يزل عالماً بذاته وصفاته ، وما يحدثه من مخلوقاته (۱) ومهما حدثت المخلوقات لم يحدث له علم بها ، بل حصلت مكشوفة له بالعلم الأزلي(٢) إذ لو خلق لنا علم بقدوم زيد عند طلوع الشمس ودام ذلك علم تقديراً حتى طلعت الشمس لكان قدوم زيد عند طلوع الشمس معلوماً لنا بذلك من غير تجدد علم آخر (۳). فهكذا ينبغي أن يفهم قدم علم الله تعالى

(۱) مخلوقاته الكائنة في علمه، وهذا ضروري أيضاً ، فإنه تعالى لا يتصف بحادث لأنه لو جاز إتصافه بالحوادث لجاز النقصان عليه والنقصان عليه باطل ، ومحال أجماعاً بيان اللزوم إن ذلك الحادث إن كان من صفات الكمال كان الخلو عنه مع جواز الإتصاف به نقصاً وقد خلا عنه قبل حدوثه وإن لم يكن من صفات الكمال إمتنع إتصاف الواجب به ، لأن كل ما يتصف به الواجب يكون كمالاً ، وأيضاً لو إتصف بالحادث لكان قابلا له ، ولو كان قابلا له لما خلا عنه ، أو عن نهده ، وإلا لزم الترجيح من غير مرجح ، وضد الحادث حادث ، وما لا يخلو عن الحادث حادث ، وأيضاً لو إتصف بالحادث لكان محلاً للإنفصال وكل منفصل مفتقر إلى ما إنفصل عنه ، وكل مفتقر ليس بواجب الوجود ، وقد فرض واجباً هذا خلق
(۲) والأزلي : لأبتداء لوجوده كما أنه تعالى كان عالماً في الأزل بأنه سيخلق العالم ثم لما خلقه فيما لا يزال كان عالماً بأنه خلقه والتجدد على المعلوم لا على العلم
(۳) وعلم الله تعالى بالأشياء قديم فإستحال لقدمه عزوبه ، لأنه عدمه وما ثبت قدمه إستحال عدمه .
The Eighth Principle: Pre-eternity of Knowledge (qidam al-ʿilm)
That His knowledge is pre-eternal; He has never ceased to be knowing of His essence, His attributes, and the created things He brings into existence (1). Whenever created things come into existence, no new knowledge occurs for Him; rather, they become disclosed to Him through the pre-eternal knowledge (2). For if a knowledge of the coming of Zayd at sunrise were created in us, and that knowledge persisted until sunrise, then the coming of Zayd at sunrise would be known to us by that [knowledge] without the renewal of another knowledge (3). Thus should the pre-eternity of Allah’s knowledge be understood.

(1) His created things that are in His knowledge. This is also necessary, for He, exalted, is not described by an originated thing, because if it were permissible for Him to be described by originated things, then deficiency would be permissible for Him, and deficiency is invalid for Him, impossible by consensus. The demonstration of the entailment: if that originated thing is among the attributes of perfection, then being devoid of it despite the possibility of being described by it is deficiency, and He was devoid of it before its occurrence; and if it is not among the attributes of perfection, then it is impossible for the Necessary Being to be described by it, because everything by which the Necessary Being is described is perfection. Also, if He were described by an originated thing, He would be receptive to it, and if He were receptive to it, He could not be free from it or from its opposite, otherwise there would be preponderance without a preponderator. The opposite of the originated is originated, and whatever is not free from the originated is originated. Also, if He were described by an originated thing, He would be a locus of separation, and everything that is separated is dependent on what it is separated from, and everything dependent is not necessary of existence, but He was assumed to be necessary.
(2) And 'pre-eternal' (al-azalī) means the beginninglessness of its existence, just as He, exalted, was knowing in pre-eternity that He would create the world, then when He created it in what is without end, He was knowing that He created it, and the renewal occurs on the known, not on the knowledge.
(3) And Allah’s knowledge of things is pre-eternal, so it is impossible for it to disappear due to its pre-eternity, because disappearance is non-existence, and what is established as pre-eternal is impossible to cease.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage defines the Eighth Principle: the pre-eternity of God’s knowledge (ʿilm). It states that God’s knowledge is pre-eternal (qadīm), always existing, and that He knows His essence, attributes, and all created things. When things come into existence, no new knowledge arises in God; rather, they are known through His pre-eternal knowledge from the beginning. To illustrate, Ghazali gives an analogy: if we have a created knowledge that Zayd will come at sunrise, and that knowledge persists until sunrise, then we know his coming without needing a new knowledge. Similarly, God’s eternal knowledge of all events suffices. The footnotes elaborate: (1) argues that God cannot be described by originated attributes because that would imply deficiency or change, and the Necessary Being must be perfect; (2) explains that the pre-eternal knowledge is beginningless and that change pertains to the known objects, not to the knowledge itself; (3) emphasizes that because God’s knowledge is pre-eternal, it cannot cease to exist.

Key Terms
علم قديم
أزلي
قدم
تجدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge changes or increases when new events occur.
Correction: God's knowledge is pre-eternal and unchanging; events are known from eternity without any change in knowledge.

Checkpoint
What does the analogy of Zayd coming at sunrise illustrate?
A) That knowledge of a future event can persist without renewal.
B) That created knowledge is eternal.
C) That God’s knowledge is created.
D) That events change God’s knowledge.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is pre-eternal, unchanging, and knows all events from eternity without renewal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge is pre-eternal and unchanging, then every choice you have ever made and will ever make was eternally known to Him. How does this reality affect your view of your own decisions? Do you live as if your choices matter, knowing they are already known? Reflect on an area where you have acted carelessly, and commit to remembering that your actions are known from eternity.
?
Consider the analogy of Zayd's arrival. Your persistent knowledge of a planned event suffices without new knowledge. But God's knowledge is perfect from eternity. How does this challenge your tendency to think that God is 'surprised' by your sins or struggles? What does it mean for your relationship with Him that He knew every failure before you were born?
?
The passage argues that God cannot be described by originated attributes because that would imply deficiency. Think of a time you wished God would 'change His mind' about something in your life. How does the pre-eternity of His knowledge reframe that desire? What does it teach you about trusting His eternal plan?

The Pre-Eternity of Divine Will
Ghazali asserts the pre-eternity of Divine will with a brief argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic theology affirms that God's will is pre-eternal and not created in time.

Core Idea
God's will is eternal—if it were created, God would be a place for events.

This lesson introduces the Ninth Principle from Ghazali's Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id. The core claim is that God's will is pre-eternal, not originated. Ghazali presents a brief argument: if the will were created, God would be a locus of events, which is impossible for the Creator. Let's delve into this foundational doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the concise style of Ghazali's principle and the incomplete final sentence.
The text breaks off after 'ولو حدثت في'—this is due to manuscript variation.
2
Identify the main claim and argument
The passage states that God's will is pre-eternal and that it relates to events according to His knowledge. The brief argument is that if the will were originated, God would become a locus of events. Reflect on why that is impossible for God.
3
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this principle matters: it protects God's transcendence and establishes that all events are determined by His eternal will, not by any temporal change in Him.
This connects to broader themes of qadar and divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل التاسع
·
أن إرادته قديمة ، وهي في القدم تعلقت باحداث الحوادث في أوقاتها اللائقة بها على وفق سبق العلم الأزلي (٤) ، إذ لو كانت حادثة لصار محل الحوادث ولو حدثت في

(٤) بمعنى أن كل كائن في الوجود من خير وشر وطاعة ومعصية بإرادته ، وأن كل ما تتعلق به إرادته يكون لا محالة وهو معنى ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن ؛
ثم إن التعلق هو كون الصفة بحيث يكون لها منسوب يرتبط بها إرتباط المتضايقين وهو على
قسمين :
صلاحي إن لم يكن المنسوب لها موجوداً في الخارج، وتنجيزى إن كان موجوداً .
The Ninth Principle
·
That His will is pre-eternal, and in pre-eternity it relates to the bringing into existence of events at their appropriate times according to the prior pre-eternal knowledge (4). For if it were originated, He would become a locus of events, and if it occurred in [the text breaks off]

(4) Meaning that everything that comes into existence, good or evil, obedience or disobedience, is by His will, and that everything to which His will relates occurs necessarily; this is the meaning of 'What Allah wills comes to be, and what He does not will does not come to be.' Then, the relation (taʿalluq) is that the attribute is such that it has a correlate that relates to it like the relation of two correlatives. It is of two kinds: preparatory (ṣalāḥī) if the correlate does not exist externally, and executive (tanjīzī) if it exists.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage introduces the Ninth Principle: the pre-eternity of God’s will (irāda). It states that God’s will is pre-eternal (qadīma) and that in that pre-eternity it relates (taʿalluq) to the creation of events at their appropriate times, in accordance with His prior pre-eternal knowledge. The author argues that if the will were originated (ḥāditha), God would become a locus of events, which is impossible. The text breaks off after 'if it occurred in' (wa-law ḥadathat fī). The footnote (4) expands on the meaning: everything that exists, whether good or evil, is by God’s will, and what He wills must occur. It then defines the relation (taʿalluq) of the will to its objects as a necessary correlation (irtibāṭ al-mutaḍāyifayn), and divides it into two types: preparatory (ṣalāḥī) when the object does not yet exist externally, and executive (tanjīzī) when the object exists.

حادثة

Correction: This principle establishes that God's will is eternal and unchanging; if it were originated, God would be a locus of events, which contradicts His transcendence.

Checkpoint

C) It would imply multiple wills.

Key Takeaway
God's will must be pre-eternal because an originated will would make God subject to change and temporality.

?
If God were a 'locus of events,' He would be affected by your actions—changed by your sins or pleased by your obedience. But this principle declares He is beyond such change. How does that affect the way you approach Him in repentance? Do you repent because you believe He will change His attitude toward you, or because you acknowledge His eternal will and mercy?

The Two Types of Divine Will's Relation
Ghazali explains the meaning of Divine will and its two types of relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of relation (ta'alluq) of God's will: preparatory and executive.

Core Idea
God's will relates to things in two ways: preparing future events or executing present ones.

In the footnote to the Ninth Principle, Ghazali elaborates on the meaning of God's will. He explains that everything occurs by His will, and defines two kinds of relation: preparatory (for what does not yet exist) and executive (for what already exists). This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the footnote passage
Read the Arabic footnote (4) carefully. It is a complete explanation of the meaning of God's will and the two types of relation. Follow along with the translation.
This footnote is integral to understanding the principle.
2
Understand the meaning of ta'alluq
Ghazali defines ta'alluq (relation) as a necessary correlation between the attribute (will) and its object, like two correlatives. Recognize that this relation is not a change in God but a description of how His will pertains to creation.
3
Differentiate between preparatory and executive
Preparatory (ṣalāḥī) applies when the object does not yet exist externally—it is a relation of preparing for future existence. Executive (tanjīzī) applies when the object already exists—it is a relation of confirming or sustaining. Consider examples: God's will for the creation of the world before it existed was preparatory; His will for its continued existence is executive.

The Eternity of Divine Knowledge: Definition and Analogy
Ghazali defines eternal knowledge and gives an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's knowledge is eternal and does not increase or change with the occurrence of events, as illustrated by Ghazali's analogy.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is eternal; events do not add to it but are disclosed by it.

Imam Ghazali begins the eighth principle by affirming that God's knowledge is eternal. He then offers a compelling analogy: if a created knowledge of a future event were placed in us and persisted until the event, we would not need a new act of knowledge. This illustrates how God's eternal knowledge encompasses all events without renewal.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the opening statement: 'His knowledge is eternal, and He has never ceased being knowing by His essence and attributes.' Notice the universal scope.
The phrase 'by His essence' is key to understanding that knowledge is not acquired.
2
Understand the analogy
Read the analogy about Zayd's coming at sunrise. Identify how a persistent created knowledge can render a new event known without a new knowledge.
The analogy is meant to be a simile for God, though God's knowledge is not created.
3
Apply to God
Reflect on how God's eternal knowledge covers all events. Understand that no new knowledge occurs; events are disclosed by the same eternal knowledge.
This is the core point of the passage.
